reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Model that performs convolution, group normalization, scaling, max pooling, and clamping.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, num_groups, scale_shape, maxpool_kernel_size, clamp_min, clamp_max):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size)
        self.group_norm = nn.GroupNorm(num_groups, out_channels)
        self.scale = nn.Parameter(torch.ones(scale_shape))
        self.maxpool = nn.MaxPool2d(kernel_size=maxpool_kernel_size)
        self.clamp_min = clamp_min
        self.clamp_max = clamp_max

    def forward(self, x):
        """
        Args:
            x: Input tensor of shape (batch_size, in_channels, height, width).
        Returns:
            Output tensor of shape (batch_size, out_channels, height', width').
        """
        x = self.conv(x)
        x = self.group_norm(x)
        x = x * self.scale
        x = self.maxpool(x)
        x = torch.clamp(x, self.clamp_min, self.clamp_max)
        return x

batch_size = 128
in_channels = 8
out_channels = 64
height, width = 128, 128 
kernel_size = 3
num_groups = 16
scale_shape = (out_channels, 1, 1)
maxpool_kernel_size = 4
clamp_min = 0.0
clamp_max = 1.0

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, height, width)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, num_groups, scale_shape, maxpool_kernel_size, clamp_min, clamp_max]

We need to produce a ModelNew class that replaces the reference model's forward computation with a fused custom CUDA kernel. The model consists of Conv2d -> GroupNorm -> scale (broadcasted multiply) -> MaxPool2d -> Clamp. Key observations: - The conv, group norm, scale, maxpool, and clamp can potentially be fused. - However, since we can write custom CUDA kernels, and the performance is crucial, we need to optimize. - The convolution itself is likely the heaviest operation; perhaps PyTorch's cudnn implementation is already very fast for conv. Fusing group norm, scaling, maxpool, and clamp after conv might provide speedup by reducing kernel launch overhead and memory traffic. - But can we fuse conv? Fusing conv with normalization and pooling is complex. For a small kernel (3x3) and moderate sizes, maybe we can implement a fused kernel that loads input tiles, performs conv, computes per-group mean/var (for group norm), then applies scale, maxpool, and clamp all in one go? That would be quite complex because group norm requires global per-group statistics, and maxpool over 4x4 reduces spatial dimensions, which would need careful tile handling. - An alternative: Keep conv as cudnn (PyTorch conv2d), but fuse the rest (group norm + scale + maxpool + clamp) into a single kernel. This reduces memory read/writes between intermediate tensors. Since conv output is relatively large (batch=128, out_channels=64, H=W=128 => 128*64*128*128 ~ 134M elements, ~536 MB fp32). Actually 128*64*128*128 = 134,217,728 elements. At fp32, that's 512 MB. That's a lot. On modern GPUs (A100/H100), memory load instructions can handle some striding, but coalescing works best with contiguous or small stride. Actually, with 32 threads, each reading 4 bytes at stride 16 bytes, the total span is 512 bytes. This will be serviced by multiple cache lines or memory transactions. No, `__shfl_down_sync` works within a warp. For block_size > 32, we need a two-level reduction or a loop over warps. Actually we can do warp shuffle within each warp, then reduce across warps using shared memory. Since block_size is 256 (8 warps), we need to: 1. Reduce within warp using shuffle. 2. Store warp sum to shared memory. 3. One warp reads shared memory and reduces. Let's implement a generic block reduce: ```cpp __inline__ __device__ float warpReduceSum(float val) { for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); return val; } __inline__ __device__ float blockReduceSum(float val) { static __shared__ float shared[32]; int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int wid = threadIdx.x / 32; val = warpReduceSum(val); if (lane == 0) shared[wid] = val; __syncthreads(); val = (threadIdx.x < blockDim.x / 32) ? shared[lane] : 0.0f; if (wid == 0) val = warpReduceSum(val); return val; } ``` Then in kernel: ```cpp float sum = 0.0f; for (int i = tid; i < count; i += blockDim.x) { sum += group_ptr[i]; } sum = blockReduceSum(sum); __shared__ float s_mean; if (threadIdx.x == 0) { s_mean = sum / count; mean[blockIdx.x] = s_mean; } __syncthreads(); float m = s_mean; float sum_sq = 0.0f; for (int i = tid; i < count; i += blockDim.x) { float diff = group_ptr[i] - m; sum_sq += diff * diff; } sum_sq = blockReduceSum(sum_sq); if (threadIdx.x == 0) { float var = sum_sq / count; inv_std[blockIdx.x] = rsqrtf(var + eps); } ``` This looks solid. - Note: Input pointer `group_ptr` points to the start of the group in NCHW. Since group channels are contiguous, and spatial within each channel is contiguous, `group_ptr[i]` is fine. Coalescing: consecutive threads read consecutive `i` values, so consecutive memory locations. Perfect. - Kernel 2: ```cpp __global__ void fuse_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ mean, const float* __restrict__ inv_std, const float* __restrict__ gamma, const float* __restrict__ beta, const float* __restrict__ scale, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out, int num_groups, int pool_size, float clamp_min, float clamp_max) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * C * H_out * W_out; if (idx >= total) return; // decompose idx into n, c, h_out, w_out int w_out = idx % W_out; int tmp = idx / W_out; int h_out = tmp % H_out; tmp = tmp / H_out; int c = tmp % C; int n = tmp / C; int g = c / (C / num_groups); int stat_idx = n * num_groups + g; float m = mean[stat_idx]; float istd = inv_std[stat_idx]; float g_val = gamma[c]; float b_val = beta[c]; float s_val = scale[c]; float max_val = -FLT_MAX; int h_base = h_out * pool_size; int w_base = w_out * pool_size; const float* __restrict__ inp_ptr = input + n * C * H * W + c * H * W; for (int dh = 0; dh < pool_size; ++dh) { int h = h_base + dh; for (int dw = 0; dw < pool_size; ++dw) { int w = w_base + dw; float x = inp_ptr[h * W + w]; float y = (x - m) * istd; y = y * g_val + b_val; y = y * s_val; if (y > max_val) max_val = y; } } max_val = fminf(fmaxf(max_val, clamp_min), clamp_max); output[idx] = max_val; } ``` Wait, the output indexing: we should write output as NCHW contiguous. Then h_out = tmp % H_out. Then tmp = tmp / H_out = idx / (W_out * H_out). Then c = tmp % C. offset > 0; offset /= 2) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); } return val; } __inline__ __device__ float block_reduce_sum(float val) { static __shared__ float shared[32]; int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; float m = s_mean; float sum_sq = 0.0f; for (int i = tid; i < count; i += blockDim.x) { float diff = group_ptr[i] - m; sum_sq += diff * diff; } sum_sq = block_reduce_sum(sum_sq); if (threadIdx.x == 0) { float var = sum_sq / count; // clamp small negative values caused by rounding error to 0 if (var < 0.0f) var = 0.0f; inv_std[blockIdx.x] = rsqrtf(var + eps); } } __global__ void fuse_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ mean, const float* __restrict__ inv_std, const float* __restrict__ gamma, const float* __restrict__ beta, const float* __restrict__ scale, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out, int num_groups, int pool_size, float clamp_min, float clamp_max) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; float x = inp_ptr[h * W + w]; float y = (x - m) * istd; y = y * g_val + b_val; y = y * s_val; if (y > max_val) max_val = y; } } max_val = fminf(fmaxf(max_val, clamp_min), clamp_max); output[idx] = max_val; } void forward( at::Tensor input, at::Tensor gamma, at::Tensor beta, at::Tensor scale, float eps, int num_groups, int pool_size, float clamp_min, float clamp_max, at::Tensor output) { // Ensure tensors are contiguous and on CUDA TORCH_CHECK(input.is_cuda(), "input must be a CUDA tensor"); turn 2 · 1 tool call(s) · 13.6s · 8,686 chars (click to expand)
turn 2llm latency: 13.6s
reasoning
The issue is that the `cpp_sources` file gets compiled first but `forward` function in `cuda_sources` is not visible. The `functions` list is used to auto-generate pybindings from whatever is available. If `cpp_sources` is empty and we only put CUDA code in `cuda_sources`, it seems `forward` isn't being recognized because no header. Actually the error says `forward` not declared in scope in `main.cpp`. The `load_inline` auto-generates a `main.cpp` that tries to bind `forward`, but the declaration is not visible because forward is defined in `cuda.cu`. We need to include a header/prototype declaration of `forward` in the `cpp_sources`. Or we can just put a forward declaration in `cpp_sources` that references the CUDA function. Looking at torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline docs, if we have functions in cuda_sources, we can put an extern declaration for them in cpp_sources.
call → compile_kernel
callcompile_kernel { "kernel_code": "import torch\nimport torch.nn as nn\nfrom torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline\n\ncpp_source = r'''\n#include <torch/extension.h>\nvoid forward(at::Tensor, at::Tensor, at::Tensor, at::Tensor, float, int, int, float, float, at::Tensor);\n'''\n\ncuda_source = r'''\n#include <torch/extension.h>\n#include <cuda_runtime.h>\n#include <float.h>\n\n__inline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) {\n for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) {\n val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset);\n }\n return val;\n}\n\n__inline__ __device__ float block_reduce_sum(float val) {\n __shared__ float shared[32];\n int lane = threadIdx.x % 32;\n int wid = threadIdx.x / 32;\n val = warp_reduce_sum(val);\n if (lane == 0) {\n shared[wid] = val;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n val = (threadIdx.x < blockDim.x / 32) ? shared[lane] : 0.0f;\n if (wid == 0) {\n val = warp_reduce_sum(val);\n }\n return val;\n}\n\n__global__ void compute_group_stats_kernel(\n const float* __restrict__ input,\n float* __restrict__ mean,\n float* __restrict__ inv_std,\n int N, int C, int H, int W, int num_groups, float eps) {\n int n = blockIdx.x / num_groups;\n int g = blockIdx.x % num_groups;\n int tid = threadIdx.x;\n int c_per_group = C / num_groups;\n int count = c_per_group * H * W;\n const float* group_ptr = input + n * C * H * W + g * c_per_group * H * W;\n\n float sum = 0.0f;\n for (int i = tid; i < count; i += blockDim.x) {\n sum += group_ptr[i];\n }\n sum = block_reduce_sum(sum);\n\n __shared__ float s_mean;\n if (threadIdx.x == 0) {\n s_mean = sum / count;\n mean[blockIdx.x] = s_mean;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n float m = s_mean;\n float sum_sq = 0.0f;\n for (int i = tid; i < count; i += blockDim.x) {\n float diff = group_ptr[i] - m;\n sum_sq += diff * diff;\n }\n sum_sq = block_reduce_sum(sum_sq);\n\n if (threadIdx.x == 0) {\n float var = sum_sq / count;\n if (var < 0.0f) var = 0.0f;\n inv_std[blockIdx.x] = rsqrtf(var + eps);\n }\n}\n\n__global__ void fuse_kernel(\n const float* __restrict__ input,\n const float* __restrict__ mean,\n const float* __restrict__ inv_std,\n const float* __restrict__ gamma,\n const float* __restrict__ beta,\n const float* __restrict__ scale,\n float* __restrict__ output,\n int N, int C, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out, int num_groups,\n int pool_size, float clamp_min, float clamp_max) {\n int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;\n int total = N * C * H_out * W_out;\n if (idx >= total) return;\n\n int w_out = idx % W_out;\n int tmp = idx / W_out;\n int h_out = tmp % H_out;\n tmp = tmp / H_out;\n int c = tmp % C;\n int n = tmp / C;\n\n int g = c / (C / num_groups);\n int stat_idx = n * num_groups + g;\n\n float m = mean[stat_idx];\n float istd = inv_std[stat_idx];\n float g_val = gamma[c];\n float b_val = beta[c];\n float s_val = scale[c];\n\n const float* __restrict__ inp_ptr = input + n * C * H * W + c * H * W;\n int h_base = h_out * pool_size;\n int w_base = w_out * pool_size;\n\n float max_val = -FLT_MAX;\n for (int dh = 0; dh < pool_size; ++dh) {\n int h = h_base + dh;\n for (int dw = 0; dw < pool_size; ++dw) {\n int w = w_base + dw;\n float x = inp_ptr[h * W + w];\n float y = (x - m) * istd;\n y = y * g_val + b_val;\n y = y * s_val;\n if (y > max_val) max_val = y;\n }\n }\n\n max_val = fminf(fmaxf(max_val, clamp_min), clamp_max);\n output[idx] = max_val;\n}\n\nvoid forward(\n at::Tensor input,\n at::Tensor gamma,\n at::Tensor beta,\n at::Tensor scale,\n float eps,\n int num_groups,\n int pool_size,\n float clamp_min,\n float clamp_max,\n at::Tensor output) {\n TORCH_CHECK(input.is_cuda(), \"input must be a CUDA tensor\");\n TORCH_CHECK(gamma.is_cuda(), \"gamma must be a CUDA tensor\");\n TORCH_CHECK(beta.is_cuda(), \"beta must be a CUDA tensor\");\n TORCH_CHECK(scale.is_cuda(), \"scale must be a CUDA tensor\");\n TORCH_CHECK(output.is_cuda(), \"output must be a CUDA tensor\");\n\n int N = input.size(0);\n int C = input.size(1);\n int H = input.size(2);\n int W = input.size(3);\n int H_out = output.size(2);\n int W_out = output.size(3);\n\n TORCH_CHECK(C % num_groups == 0, \"out_channels must be divisible by num_groups\");\n\n auto options = input.options();\n auto mean = at::empty({N * num_groups}, options);\n auto inv_std = at::empty({N * num_groups}, options);\n\n dim3 block1(256);\n dim3 grid1(N * num_groups);\n compute_group_stats_kernel<<<grid1, block1>>>(\n input.data_ptr<float>(), mean.data_ptr<float>(), inv_std.data_ptr<float>(),\n N, C, H, W, num_groups, eps);\n\n int total = N * C * H_out * W_out;\n dim3 block2(256);\n dim3 grid2((total + block2.x - turn 5 · 1 tool call(s) · 155.7s · 86,235 chars (click to expand)
turn 5llm latency: 155.7s
reasoning
The profiling shows that the kernel is memory-bound with very poor global load coalescing. The sectors/req is 24.4, which is terrible. This means each thread is loading data that spans many cache lines, causing massive bandwidth waste. The problem is that in my fuse_kernel, each thread iterates over H*W for a single channel/group, and in the loop `for (int i = tid; i < count; i += blockDim.x)` the stride leads to uncoalesced access within a warp. Specifically, threads in a warp access elements separated by blockDim.x, which is 256. For float32, each thread reads 4 bytes, and with a stride of 256 floats (1024 bytes), consecutive threads access addresses 1024 bytes apart, which are in different sectors (each sector is 32 bytes). reasoning
The profile showed: - Kernel is memory-bound with terrible global load coalescing (24.4 sectors/req) - The fused kernel is very register-heavy (327 regs/thread), which causes low occupancy - Need to restructure memory access to be contiguous per thread/warp The problem: - The fused kernel iterates over `pool_size x pool_size` region per output pixel. Since each thread handles a different output location, and consecutive threads handle different `(n, c, h_out, w_out)` coordinates, the memory access pattern is highly uncoalesced - different threads access completely different spatial locations. A much better approach: use shared memory tiling to load tiles of the input into shared memory, then the threads within a block can cooperatively compute the maxpool. But maybe a simpler restructuring that improves coalescing: Instead of assigning each thread to one output pixel, we should assign each thread block to a tile of output, and within that tile, have threads cooperate to load the corresponding input tile into shared memory in a coalesced way. The input dimensions after conv: N=128, C=64, H=126, W=126 (since kernel_size=3, stride=1, padding=0: 128-3+1=126) Pool size=4, output: H_out=31, W_out=31 (since floor(126/4)=31) Wait, H_out = H // pool? The reference uses MaxPool2d(kernel_size=4) with default stride=kernel_size=4 and padding=0. For 126x126 input, the output is floor((126-4)/4)+1 = floor(122/4)+1 = 30+1 = 31. And 126 is divisible by... wait 126/4 = 31.5, not an integer. The original reference uses nn.MaxPool2d(kernel_size=4). Let me check how PyTorch computes output size for MaxPool2d: output_size = floor((input_size + 2*padding - dilation*(kernel_size-1) - 1) / stride + 1) With padding=0, dilation=1, stride=4, kernel_size=4: H_out = floor((126 - 4) / 4 + 1) = floor(122/4 + 1) = floor(30.5 + 1) = floor(31.5) = 31 So my code used H_out = H // pool = 126 // 4 = 31. That happens to work. My current kernel layout: Each thread gets 1 output element. Total = 128 * 64 * 31 * 31 = 7,878,272 threads. block=256, grid=30775. Every thread accesses a 4x4 region in input. The base address for thread idx is `inp_ptr + (h_out*4)*W + (w_out*4)` where h_out and w_out vary per thread. Adjacent threads have consecutive `w_out`, so they access spatial locations separated by 4 in width. That's not great but could be better than I thought. Wait, consecutive threads with idx differing by 1 have w_out differing by 1. So they access input rows starting at w_base and w_base+4. The row data is contiguous but offset by 4. Let me think... Actually, consecutive threads in a warp (32 threads) will have: thread 0: w_out=0, h_out=0 thread 1: w_out=1, h_out=0 ... thread 30: w_out=30, h_out=0 thread 31: w_out=0, h_out=1 For thread i: accesses input at row h_out*4, columns w_out*4 to w_out*4+3. Consecutive threads access columns 0,4,8,12,... This means each thread accesses a different 4-word segment. The distance between thread 0 and thread 1 accesses is 4 floats = 16 bytes. So a warp reads 32 separate 16-byte regions? Actually if each thread reads 4 floats (16 bytes), and the starting addresses differ by 16 bytes, then the addresses are 16, 32, 48, 64 bytes apart from thread 0. Wait: thread 0: base address A thread 1: base address A + 16 thread 2: base address A + 32 ... thread 31: base address A + 496 But all threads read 16 bytes starting at these addresses. In memory, these are contiguous 512-byte regions! A warp of 32 threads will collectively read 512 bytes = 32 sectors of 32 bytes each (on H100, sector is 32 bytes). Actually if memory transactions are 128 bytes, then threads 0-7 would read addresses A, A+16, A+32, A+48, A+64, A+80, A+96, A+112 - that's a contiguous 128-byte block. But each thread reads 16 bytes. In a single 128-byte transaction, can 8 threads each read 16 bytes starting at offsets 0,16,...,112? Yes! That would be perfectly coalesced. Then thread 8 reads A+128, thread 9 reads A+144, etc. In fact, threads 0-31 would read a contiguous 512-byte span. This should be highly coalesced! So why is the profiler saying 24.4 sectors/req? Hmm, because the blockDim.x is 256, and consecutive threads map to increasing `idx`, which increments `w_out` by 1. But when `w_out` wraps from 30 to 0, `h_out` increments. So thread 31: h_out=1, w_out=0. It reads memory at (h=4, w=0), while thread 30 reads (h=0, w=120). The distance between these is huge! So within a warp, not all 32 threads have contiguous addresses. Specifically, the mapping from thread to index is: idx = ((n*C + c)*H_out + h_out)*W_out + w_out For a fixed n, c, h_out, w_out runs 0..30. Thread 0-30: same n, c, h_out=0, w_out=0..30. This breaks coalescing! A warp containing threads 0-31 (or more precisely, a warp of 32 threads with indices 0..31) will have addresses spanning a huge range. Specifically, threads in a warp come from the same block. blockDim.x=256. Warp 0 contains threads 0..31 with w_out = 0..31, but W_out=31, so actually: Warp 0: threads 0..31 correspond to w_out=0..30 then h_out increments. Wait, the 32 threads in a warp are not necessarily consecutive in the same way. In CUDA, threads in a warp are consecutive threadIdx.x values if blockDim.x >= 32. So warp 0 = threadIdx.x 0..31. And blockIdx.x determines the starting `idx`. Let me consider blockIdx.x = 0: threadIdx.x 0 -> idx = 0 -> n=0, c=0, h=0, w=0 threadIdx.x 1 -> idx = 1 -> n=0, c=0, h=0, w=1 ... threadIdx.x 30 -> idx = 30 -> n=0, c=0, h=0, w=30 threadIdx.x 31 -> idx = 31 -> n=0, c=0, h=1, w=0 So within warp 0, threads 0..30 access w_base = 0, 4, 8, ..., 120 in row h=0. No, 62 // 31 = 2, remainder 0. So h=2, w=0. And threadIdx.x 63 -> h=2, w=1. So a warp can contain threads from different h_out values. In fact, every 31 indices, h_out increments. This means within a warp, addresses are generally within a few rows, but the exact distribution depends on the thread index. The key observation: each thread accesses a 4x4 tile. Within a warp, different threads access different tiles. The memory distance between thread i and i+1 is usually 16 bytes (4 floats) in width, but every 31 threads, there's a big jump (to the next row). But the rows themselves are 126 floats apart = 504 bytes. In a warp of 32 threads: threads 0..30 access row 0 or rows around it (actually all in row 0 if W_out >= 32, but W_out=31). Thread 31 access row 4. threads 0..30: h_base = 0, w_base = threadIdx.x * 4 thread 31: h_base = 4, w_base = 0 So addresses within the warp are: thread i (0<=i<=30): base_addr + 0*W + i*4 = base_addr + i*4 thread 31: base_addr + 4*W + 0 = base_addr + 504 These are spaced out by 4 bytes (1 float) for threads 0..30, but thread 31 is at +504, which is far away. However, a warp issues memory requests for all threads. The ideal case for a 128-byte transaction is that addresses fall within the same 128-byte aligned segment. For threads 0..30, we have addresses base, base+4, base+8, ..., base+120. That's 31 addresses spanning 124 bytes. Plus thread 31 at base+504. The warp needs at least two transactions: one for threads 0..30 (and maybe more depending on alignment) and one for thread 31 (and maybe some other threads if address span is larger). Wait, there are 32 threads in a warp. Thread 31 accesses a completely different cache line. So one warp issues multiple memory transactions - one for the first 31 threads covering roughly 124 bytes (which fits in 1-2 128-byte cache lines) and one for thread 31 covering its cache line. But thread 31 also reads 4 floats = 16 bytes. It might share a cache line with thread 0 of another warp? No, because warp execution is SIMT. So per warp, we might have ~3 transactions (for 31 threads contiguous + 1 separate). And each thread reads 16 bytes. Total bytes requested = 32 * 16 = 512 bytes. If there are ~3 transactions of 128 bytes = 384 bytes... wait, no. A transaction can be up to 128 bytes. Threads 0..30 request 124 bytes of contiguous data, which could be served by 1 transaction (if aligned) or 2. Thread 31 requests 16 bytes at offset 504, which could be in a different 128-byte line. That might require 1 more transaction. Total ~3 transactions = 384 bytes transferred for 512 bytes requested. That's actually not terrible. But there are 4 rows per thread, each row being 4 floats. The above analysis was just for the first row. For each thread, it reads dh=0,1,2,3. For dh=0, threads 0..30 are in row 0, thread 31 is in row 4. For dh=1, thread 0..30 are in row 1, thread 31 in row 5. So each warp spreads across 5 different rows. That's 5*126*4 = 2520 bytes of separation just for the row dimension. Combined with 32 threads, the warp accesses 32 tiles, each tile is 4x4, scattered across a relatively large area. Ah, I see. So the memory access pattern is very scattered across rows. For dh=0: - threads 0..30 access row h=0, columns 0..120 in steps of 4 - thread 31 accesses row h=4, column 0 For dh=1: - thread 0..30 access row h=1, columns 0..120 - thread 31 accesses row h=5, column 0 So across the warp, we touch rows 0,1,2,3,4,5 (and more as we go to other warps). That's already 6 rows. Each row is 126*4=504 bytes. So the addresses span a few KB. To improve coalescing, we should use shared memory tiling. Or we can transpose the loop: have each thread handle multiple output pixels but along the C dimension, or process output pixels in a way that threads in a warp work on the same spatial region. Actually, a much better design: Instead of one thread per output pixel, use one thread block per output tile (e.g. 8x8 or 16x16 output pixels). Within the block, threads cooperatively load the corresponding input tile into shared memory. Then each thread computes max pool on the 4x4 region from shared memory. For example, tile the output into 8x8 blocks. Each output tile corresponds to an input tile of size (8*4) x (8*4) = 32x32. But the coalescing issue suggests we can do even better. Let me fix the coalescing by using a tiled shared memory approach. The main idea: - In the fused kernel, instead of each thread reading scattered 4x4 regions directly from global memory, we load tiles into shared memory. - But we need to be careful about the overhead. An alternative simpler fix: change the loop ordering. Currently each thread handles 1 output pixel. We can have each thread handle multiple output pixels across the channel dimension (C) instead, because for a given spatial location (h_out, w_out), all channels read the same spatial input tile (just different channel slices). And accesses for channels are contiguous in memory because data is NCHW. So if threads in a warp handle the same (h_out, w_out) but different c, they will read contiguous memory (consecutive channels for the same spatial position). Specifically: - Launch threads where threadIdx.x processes channels, and blockIdx.x processes spatial locations (h_out, w_out) and n. - For a fixed (n, h_out, w_out), we process all C channels. - The output layout is NCHW. So consecutive c values have consecutive memory addresses in the output. - In the input, for a fixed (n, h, w), consecutive c values have consecutive memory addresses (since NCHW). - So if 32 threads in a warp process c=0..31 for the same (n, h_base+dh, w_base+dw), they read memory addresses that differ by H*W floats (the stride between channels). The distance is H*W*4 bytes = 126*126*4 = 63504 bytes. That's not contiguous! In NCHW, channel dimension is the outermost, so memory is not contiguous across channels for the same spatial location. Adjacent channels for the same spatial pixel are separated by H*W. So that doesn't help. What about processing the same channel but different output pixels in a coalesced way? In NCHW, within a channel, spatial locations are contiguous: pixel (h,w) is at offset h*W + w. So if threads process consecutive w_out for the same h_out and c, they access: thread i: w_base = w_out[i] * pool_size. Since w_out[i] are consecutive, w_base differ by pool_size (4). So each thread reads 4 consecutive floats starting at w_base. The distance between thread i and i+1 is 4 floats = 16 bytes. If the warp reads addresses A, A+16, A+32, ..., A+480 (for 32 threads). Each thread reads 16 bytes (4 floats). The total span is 496 bytes. This could be coalesced into 4 or 5 transactions of 128 bytes. But currently, these 32 threads also jump to different rows after every 31 threads. To fix, we can have each warp handle a 2D tile of output where the dimensions are multiples that keep things contiguous. For example, if we have blockDim.x = W_out (31), and blockDim.y = something, then threads in a warp (32 threads) would have 31 threads in one row and 1 thread in the next row. Not ideal. If we pad or choose block size = 32, and process a 32x1 strip of output (width 32), then consecutive threads read consecutive w_base values, all in the same row. But W_out=31, so 32 would go to next row. Alternatively, blockDim.x = 1 thread per w, but we process a tile of (TileH x TileW) output pixels with TileW=4. Then a warp of 32 threads could process 8 output pixels in width (since 8*4=32 threads?) Wait, no. Actually, simply using shared memory tiling is the cleanest approach. Let me implement it. Tiling strategy: - Tile size: output tile = (TileH, TileW). Let's use TileH=4, TileW=8. So 32 output pixels per tile. - Corresponding input tile = 16x32 = 512 floats. Shared memory = 512*4 = 2048 bytes. - Block size = 32 threads (one per output pixel) or 64 threads. But then we still have the issue that threads 0..15 compute output pixels, while 16..63 are helper threads. In CUDA, warps are groups of 32 threads. So warp 0 would have threads 0..15 computing and 16..31 idle during compute. Warp 1 (threads 32..63) would be idle during compute. That's a lot of inactive threads but fine for loading. However, divergent warps are okay as long as the whole warp doesn't diverge. Here threads 0..15 are active and 16..31 inactive within warp 0 -> warp 0 has divergence but it's just different execution paths. Since all threads in warp 0 either compute or don't compute, this is fine. Wait, in the compute phase, only threads 0..15 should run. Threads 16..31 in warp 0 would be masked off. That's fine. The load phase: all 64 threads load. This is a good approach. But global memory load coalescing: we have 64 threads loading 256 floats. Each thread loads a float4. Thread i loads float4 at position i*4 in the shared memory buffer, which corresponds to the linearized input tile. The input tile in global memory is a sub-tensor: for a given n,c, the region [h_start : h_start+16, w_start : w_start+16]. The linear index of (dh, dw) in the tile is dh * TileW_in + dw. Thread i handles elements i*4 to i*4+3. So consecutive threads read consecutive floats! This is perfectly coalesced. Then in shared memory, each output pixel (thread 0..15) reads its 4x4 region from shared memory. Since each thread reads within the same shared memory array, there are no bank conflicts if we structure it right. Wait, shared memory bank conflicts: each thread reads a 4x4 block. For 4x4 output tile, thread (out_h, out_w) in the tile reads input region [out_h*4:(out_h+1)*4, out_w*4:(out_w+1)*4]. If threads in a warp read these regions, they access different rows and columns. In shared memory, a float array in shared memory has banks separated by 4 bytes. 32 banks. If two threads access the same bank, there's a conflict. For a 16x16 shared memory tile of floats: Address = (row * 16 + col) * 4 bytes. Bank = (address / 4) % 32 = (row * 16 + col) % 32. Thread (0,0) reads rows 0..3, cols 0..3. Addresses: row*16+col. For row=0: 0,1,2,3 -> banks 0,1,2,3 For row=1: 16,17,18,19 -> banks 16,17,18,19 For row=2: 32,33,34,35 -> banks 0,1,2,3 For row=3: 48,49,50,51 -> banks 16,17,18,19 Thread (0,1) reads cols 4..7: row=0: 4,5,6,7 -> banks 4,5,6,7 row=1: 20,21,22,23 -> banks 20,21,22,23 ... No overlaps between threads (0,0) and (0,1). But within a warp, we have threads: (0,0), (0,1), (0,2), (0,3) (1,0), (1,1), (1,2), (1,3) (2,0), (2,1), (2,2), (2,3) (3,0), (3,1), (3,2), (3,3) 16 threads in a warp all reading. Do any of them access the same bank at the same time? Thread (0,0): banks {0,1,2,3, 16,17,18,19} (8 banks per row? No, each access to shared memory can be up to 8 bytes, but 4-byte accesses go to one bank. 32 threads x 4 bytes = 128 bytes per transaction, 4 banks per transaction. So each thread reads 4 floats sequentially. In a single cycle, one thread can read one float. Actually, warp execution: all 16 threads execute simultaneously (well, in groups). At any given moment, each thread accesses one float. So we need to check if any two threads access the same bank simultaneously. Thread (0,0) at (row=0,col=0): bank 0 Thread (0,1) at (row=0,col=4): bank 4 Thread (0,2) at (row=0,col=8): bank 8 Thread (0,3) at (row=0,col=12): bank 12 Thread (1,0) at (row=4,col=0): bank (64+0)%32 = 0 -> CONFLICT with thread (0,0)! Thread (0,0) and thread (1,0) both access bank 0 but different data. That causes a 2-way bank conflict. Since there are 4 rows of output threads, and each row reads cols starting from out_w * 4. For col=0 and row=r*4 (r=0,1,2,3): address = (r*4)*16 + 0 = r*64 bank = (r*64) % 32 = 0 for all r! So all 4 threads in the first column access bank 0 for their top-left element! That's a 4-way bank conflict for the first element in each thread's region. However, because threads are reading sequentially within their loops, the exact instructions are staggered. But in warp-level, we need to check how many unique banks are accessed per instruction. Actually, each thread has a loop of 4x4=16 reads. The reads across threads in the same instruction (same step of the loop) are from the same relative offset within their 4x4 tile. For step (dh, dw): Thread (th, tw) reads shared_mem[(th*4+dh)*16 + (tw*4+dw)] Bank = ((th*4+dh)*16 + (tw*4+dw)) % 32 = (th*64 + dh*16 + tw*4 + dw) % 32 = (dh*16 + tw*4 + dw) % 32 (since th*64 is multiple of 32). So bank only depends on dh, tw, dw, not on th! For a fixed (dh, dw), all threads with the same tw will access the same bank, regardless of th. Since tw ranges 0..3 (4 values), and there are 4 threads with tw=0 (th=0,1,2,3), they all access the same bank. That's a 4-way bank conflict. For dh=0, dw=0: bank = (0 + tw*4 + 0) % 32 = tw*4 % 32. tw=0: bank 0 (4 threads access it) tw=1: bank 4 (4 threads) tw=2: bank 8 (4 threads) tw=3: bank 12 (4 threads) So 4-way conflicts. We can pad shared memory to avoid this. If we allocate with width 17 instead of 16: Address = th*4*17 + ... Bank = (th*4*17 + dh*17 + tw*4 + dw) % 32. Now th influences the bank because 17 is odd. For dh=0, dw=0: bank = (th*68 + tw*4) % 32 = (th*4 + tw*4) % 32 = (th+tw)*4 % 32. For th=0..3, tw=0..3, we get unique combinations, but some may still collide. th=0,tw=0: bank 0 th=1,tw=0: bank 4 th=2,tw=0: bank 8 th=3,tw=0: bank 12 th=0,tw=1: bank 4 -> collision with th=1,tw=0. Hmm. Let's try width=33 (17 doesn't work well). 33 is odd. bank = (th*4*33 + ...) % 32 = (th*132 + dh*33 + tw*4 + dw) % 32 = (th*4 + dh*1 + tw*4 + dw) % 32. For dh=0,dw=0: (th*4 + tw*4) % 32 = (th+tw)*4 % 32. Still the same issue. We need the stride between rows to be coprime to 32 so that consecutive rows hit different banks. Stride = width_in_floats. We want width_in_floats % 2 != 0. Width=17 gives stride=17. Row offset in banks: row * 17 % 32. 17 and 32 are coprime, so rows 0..31 hit different banks. But our tile only has 16 rows. And we care about threads reading at offsets (th*4 + dh, tw*4 + dw). The row index is r = th*4 + dh. So row offsets differ by multiples of 4. The bank for row r is (r * stride) % 32 = (th*4 + dh) * stride % 32. For th=0,1,2,3 and dh=0,1,2,3, the row indices are 0..15. Banks are r*stride % 32. With stride=17, banks are 0, 17, 2, 19, 4, 21, 6, 23, 8, 25, 10, 27, 12, 29, 14, 31. All unique! Then for a fixed row and step dw, col = tw*4 + dw. Bank = (row*stride + col) % 32. Since row gives a unique base bank, and col = tw*4+dw adds 0..15, we need to make sure no two threads with different (th,tw) map to the same bank. For dh=0,dw=0: Thread (th, tw): bank = ((th*4)*17 + tw*4) % 32 = (th*68 + tw*4) % 32 = (th*4 + tw*4) % 32 = (th+tw)*4 % 32. th,tw ranges: (0,0): 0 (0,1): 4 (0,2): 8 (0,3): 12 (1,0): 4 -> COLLISION with (0,1)! Darn. So even with unique rows, the column offset causes collisions because tw increments by 4 and th increments by 4. If we make the stride even larger coprime to 32, like 33: bank = (th*4*33 + tw*4) % 32 = (th*4 + tw*4) % 32. Same thing because 33%32=1. What about stride=5? Then th*4*5 = th*20. 20%32 = 20. (0,0): 0 (0,1): 4 (0,2): 8 (0,3): 12 (1,0): 20 (1,1): 24 (1,2): 28 (1,3): 0 -> COLLISION with (0,0)! The issue is that th*4*stride and tw*4 must produce different sums mod 32 for all th,tw in {0,1,2,3}. This means {0,4,8,12} + {0, 4*stride, 8*stride, 12*stride} mod 32 should be unique. Let S = 4*stride mod 32. We need {i*S + j*4} to be unique for i,j in {0,1,2,3}. That means S/4 must be odd (so S = 4, 12, 20, 28) and gcd(S/4, 8) = 1. If S = 4, then S/4 = 1, which is coprime to 8. Let's check S=4 (stride=1): bank = (th*4*1 + tw*4) % 32 = (th+tw)*4 % 32. This gives duplicates as shown. Wait, the set of values is {4*(i+j)} for i,j in 0..3. The sums i+j range from 0 to 6. So the banks are {0,4,8,12,16,20,24}. That's only 7 unique banks for 16 threads! So lots of conflicts. This happens because the offsets are small compared to 32. To get unique banks for all 16 threads across the tile, we need the row stride and column stride to be such that (th, tw) map to unique banks. The condition is: for any (th1, tw1) != (th2, tw2), (th1*4*stride + tw1*4) != (th2*4*stride + tw2) mod 32. This simplifies to (th1 - th2)*stride + (tw1 - tw2) != 0 mod 8 (after dividing by 4). So we need the 2D lattice generated by (stride, 1) to have no collisions in a 8x? torus. Specifically, need 4*stride and 4 to be linearly independent mod 32 in a sense that difference combinations are nonzero. Since both are multiples of 4, we work mod 8: 4*stride/4 = stride mod 8 4/4 = 1 mod 8 We need for (dh, dt) in {0,1,2,3}, not both zero: dh * (stride mod 8) + dt != 0 mod 8. If stride mod 8 = 1: dh + dt != 0 mod 8. Since dh, dt in -3..3, the sum is in -6..6, so only 0 if dh=-dt. This happens! e.g. dh=1, dt=-1. So (th=1,tw=0) and (th=0,tw=1) collide. If stride mod 8 = 3: 3*dh + dt != 0 mod 8. Check all 15 nonzero pairs... dh=1,dt=5(not valid); dh=1, dt in -3..3: 3+dt=0 -> dt=-3 (valid). So (1,0) and (0,-3)? tw difference can be -3, meaning th=1,tw=1 vs th=0,tw=-2? But tw must be in 0..3. So within 0..3, can we have dt=-3? That would require tw1=3, tw2=0. dh=th1-th2=1. So (th=1,tw=0) and (th=0,tw=3): 3*1 + (-3) = 0 mod 8? Wait, dt = tw1 - tw2. So for (th1=1,tw1=0) and (th2=0,tw2=3): dh=1, dt=-3. 3*1 + (-3) = 0 mod 8. Yes, collision. If stride mod 8 = 7: 7*dh + dt != 0. 7*1 + (-7) but dt in -3..3, no. 7*2 = 14 = 6 mod 8. 6+dt=0 -> dt=-6 (not in range). 7*3=21=5. 5+dt=0 -> dt=-5 (not in range). Negative dh: 7*(-1)=1, 1+dt=0 -> dt=-1 (valid). So (th=0,tw=1) and (th=1,tw=0): dh=-1, dt=1. 7*(-1)+1 = -6 != 0 mod 8. Good. Other combinations: dh=-2: 7*(-2)=-14=2, 2+dt=0 -> dt=-2. (th=0,tw=2) and (th=2,tw=0): dh=-2, dt=2. 2+2=4 != 0. dh=-3: 7*(-3)=-21=3, 3+dt=0 -> dt=-3. (th=0,tw=3) and (th=3,tw=0): dh=-3,dt=3. 3+3=6 != 0. Positive dh: dh=1: 7+dt=0 -> dt=1 (since 7+1=8=0). So (th=1,tw=0) and (th=0,tw=1): dh=1,dt=-1. 7*1 + (-1) = 6 != 0. Wait, for collision we need 7*dh + dt = 0 mod 8. For dh=1, dt=-1: 7-1=6 != 0. Good. For dh=1, dt=1: 7+1=0 mod 8! So (th=2,tw=0) and (th=1,tw=1): dh=1,dt=-1 -> 7-1=6. Wait I'm confusing. Let me be systematic. Consider pairs (dh, dt) in {-3,-2,-1,1,2,3}^2 but note dt range actually depends on tw values, but in principle any integer pair with |dh|<=3, |dt|<=3, not both zero. We need 7*dh + dt != 0 mod 8. dh=1: 7+dt=0 -> dt=1 (mod 8). dt can be 1. So yes, collision when dh=1, dt=1. Example: (th=1,tw=0) and (th=0,tw=-1) -- but tw can't be -1. How about (th=2,tw=1) and (th=1,tw=0)? dh=1, dt=1. 7+1=0 mod 8. Collision! So no odd stride mod 8 works because of the small range. The problem is fundamental: with a 4x4 output tile and 4x4 pool, the step sizes in shared memory are multiples of 4. Since there are 32 banks, and our tile is 16x16, the addressing pattern has periodicity that causes conflicts. However, 4-way bank conflicts are not terrible. The current version has global memory coalescing issues, which are much worse (24x wastage). Moving to shared memory will fix the global memory issue, and shared memory bank conflicts of 4-way are acceptable. So let me just use a simple shared memory approach with a padded shared memory to reduce conflicts. Since we don't need perfect bank freedom, let's try stride=17 (as I calculated earlier, rows have unique banks), and accept some column conflicts. Actually wait, for a given step in the inner loop, say dh=0,dw=0, we have 4 threads (same tw, different th) accessing the same bank. But within the loop, each thread accesses 16 values. In the first step (dh=0,dw=0), threads with same tw but different th access bank (th*4 + tw*4) % 32. For fixed tw, th=0,1,2,3 give banks 4*tw, 4*tw+4, 4*tw+8, 4*tw+12 mod 32. These are 4 DIFFERENT banks! Wait, earlier I said th*4*stride mod 32 with stride=17 is th*4. Oh right! I said the dh=0,dw=0 case, bank depends only on th and tw and (th+tw)*4. For fixed tw and varying th, banks are 4*tw, 4*tw+4, 4*tw+8, 4*tw+12. These are unique for th=0..3! So no conflict among threads with same tw and different th. Wait, then where does the conflict come from? It comes from (th=0,tw=1) and (th=1,tw=0): both have bank 4. But these are DIFFERENT threads in the warp. So at step (dh=0,dw=0), thread (0,0) accesses bank 0, thread (0,1) accesses bank 4, thread (1,0) accesses bank 4. So thread (0,1) and (1,0) conflict! They both try to read from bank 4. That's a 2-way conflict. Then how many unique banks at step (0,0)? Values of (th+tw)*4 mod 32 for th,tw in 0..3. th+tw ranges: 0,1,2,3 (sum=0:1 way; 1:2 ways; 2:3 ways; 3:4 ways; 4:3 ways; 5:2 ways; 6:1 way). Banks: 0,4,8,12,16,20,24. For bank 0: thread (0,0) -> 1 thread Bank 4: threads (0,1), (1,0) -> 2 threads Bank 8: threads (0,2), (1,1), (2,0) -> 3 threads Bank 12: threads (0,3), (1,2), (2,1), (3,0) -> 4 threads Bank 16: threads (1,3), (2,2), (3,1) -> 3 threads Bank 20: threads (2,3), (3,2) -> 2 threads Bank 24: thread (3,3) -> 1 thread So the worst conflict is 4-way at bank 12. A 4-way bank conflict means the shared memory request takes 4 cycles instead of 1. That's fine. Now for step dh=0,dw=1: bank = ((th+tw)*4 + 1) % 32. Banks: 1,5,9,13,17,21,25. Same distribution. dh=1,dw=0: bank = ((th+tw)*4 + 16) % 32. Banks: 16,20,24,28,0,4,8. Also same. dh=1,dw=1: bank = ((th+tw)*4 + 17) % 32 = ((th+tw)*4 + 1 + 16) % 32. Banks: 17,21,25,29,1,5,9. So conflicts are always at most 4-way. This is acceptable. The big win will be in global memory where we now have perfect coalescing. Let me implement the tiled version. This should be much faster than the old version. Let me implement this carefully. Kernel design: ```cuda #define TILE_H 8 #define TILE_W 8 #define POOL 4 #define TILE_H_IN (TILE_H * POOL) #define TILE_W_IN (TILE_W * POOL) #define TILE_W_IN_PADDED 33 // pad to avoid bank conflicts __global__ void fused_kernel(..., int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out, ...) { int n = blockIdx.x; int c = blockIdx.y; int tile_id = blockIdx.z; int tiles_w = (W_out + TILE_W - 1) / TILE_W; int tile_h = tile_id / tiles_w; int tile_w = tile_id % tiles_w; int h_start = tile_h * TILE_H; int w_start = tile_w * TILE_W; int num_valid_h = min(TILE_H, H_out - h_start); int num_valid_w = min(TILE_W, W_out - w_start); int num_valid_in_h = num_valid_h * POOL; int num_valid_in_w = num_valid_w * POOL; __shared__ float smem[TILE_H_IN * TILE_W_IN_PADDED]; // Load into shared memory const float* inp_n_c = input + ((n * C + c) * H) * W; int h_in_start = h_start * POOL; int w_in_start = w_start * POOL; int total_in = num_valid_in_h * num_valid_in_w; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < total_in; i += blockDim.x) { int r = i / num_valid_in_w; int cc = i % num_valid_in_w; int global_h = h_in_start + r; int global_w = w_in_start + cc; smem[r * TILE_W_IN_PADDED + cc] = inp_n_c[global_h * W + global_w]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute int tid = threadIdx.x; int out_h = tid / TILE_W; int out_w = tid % TILE_W; if (out_h < num_valid_h && out_w < num_valid_w) { int local_h_start = out_h * POOL; int local_w_start = out_w * POOL; float m = mean[n * num_groups + c / (C/num_groups)]; float istd = inv_std[...]; float g_val = gamma[c]; float b_val = beta[c]; float s_val = scale[c]; float a = istd * g_val * s_val; float offs = b_val * s_val - m * a; float max_val = -FLT_MAX; #pragma unroll for (int dh = 0; dh < POOL; ++dh) { for (int dw = 0; dw < POOL; ++dw) { float x = smem[(local_h_start + dh) * TILE_W_IN_PADDED + (local_w_start + dw)]; float y = x * a + offs; y = fminf(fmaxf(y, clamp_min), clamp_max); max_val = fmaxf(max_val, y); } } int out_idx = ((n * C + c) * H_out + (h_start + out_h)) * W_out + (w_start + out_w); output[out_idx] = max_val; } } ``` Grid: (N, C, num_tiles_z) num_tiles = ceil(H_out/TILE_H) * ceil(W_out/TILE_W). For N=128, C=64, H_out=31, W_out=31: num_tiles = 4*4 = 16. So grid = (128, 64, 16). Total blocks = 131072. Block size = 256. Shared memory = TILE_H_IN * TILE_W_IN_PADDED * sizeof(float) = 32 * 33 * 4 = 4224 bytes. Very small. Register count should be reasonable. Occupancy: With 4224 bytes smem and 256 threads, and assuming ~32 registers per thread. On H100, max blocks per SM is 32. Threads per SM = 2048. So 8 blocks per SM if 256 threads. Shared memory 4224 * 8 = 33792 bytes < 164KB. So occupancy should be high. One concern: `blockIdx.z` is limited to 65535 on older GPUs, but on Ampere/Hopper it's 65535 as well? Actually the limit for gridDim.z is 65535 for devices with compute capability < 3.0, but on modern GPUs, gridDim.z can be up to 65535 too? Wait, CUDA limits: gridDim.x up to 2^31-1, gridDim.y and gridDim.z up to 65535. Our gridDim.z = 16, which is well within the limit. Actually, blockIdx.z vs gridDim.z: modern CUDA still limits gridDim.y and gridDim.z to 65535. So we're fine. But I used a 3D grid: (N, C, num_tiles) = (128, 64, 16). That's valid. Wait, there's another optimization: we can precompute a = istd * gamma[c] * scale[c] and offs = beta[c]*scale[c] - mean*istd*gamma[c]*scale[c] on the CPU (or in a separate kernel) to save computation. One important thing: for the load loop, when loading the valid region into shared memory, with num_valid_in_w varying per tile, the accesses to `smem[r * TILE_W_IN_PADDED + cc]` have a stride of TILE_W_IN_PADDED=33 between rows. The writes to shared memory are done by threads with consecutive `i`. `i = r * num_valid_in_w + cc`. For consecutive threads with i varying by 1, `r` and `cc` change. Specifically, within a row (fixed r, cc increments), threads write to consecutive addresses in shared memory (since cc increments by 1, and shared memory index increments by 1). These addresses are in consecutive banks (since stride is 33, bank = index % 32, so consecutive cc means consecutive banks). So shared memory writes during load are bank-conflict-free! Great. For global memory loads: thread i loads `inp_n_c[global_h * W + global_w]` where global_w increments for consecutive threads within a row. Since W=126 and threads are reading consecutive columns within the same row, the global memory accesses are perfectly coalesced. But when transitioning from `cc = num_valid_in_w-1` to `cc = 0` (next row), `global_h` increments by 1, `global_w` drops back. This causes a jump, but threads are in lockstep. For a warp of 32 threads, if num_valid_in_w >= 32, then threads 0..31 will all be in the first row, reading 32 consecutive floats from global memory. If num_valid_in_w < 32, threads might span multiple rows. For boundary tiles, num_valid_in_w can be 28. Then threads 0..27 are in row 0, thread 28 is in row 1. The addresses for threads 28..31 jump to W away. This causes multiple transactions. But boundary tiles are few (about 25% of tiles), so the impact is small. To improve, we could have each warp handle an entire row of the input tile. With block size=256 and 32 cols per row, 8 rows can be loaded by one warp (256/32 = 8 warps, 32 rows / 8 warps = 4 rows per warp). Actually, let me think. If we assign each warp to load one or more full rows: tile has 32 rows. We have 8 warps. Each warp loads 4 rows. Within a warp, 32 threads load 32 floats from one row (1 float per thread). That's perfectly coalesced. Then next row: the same warp loads the next row, also perfectly coalesced. This avoids the cross-row issue within a warp. Let's do this: For load, we iterate over rows. num_rows_to_load = num_valid_in_h; for (int row = threadIdx.y; row < num_rows_to_load; row += blockDim.y) ... But we only have 1D blockDim. We can emulate: int total_rows = num_valid_in_h; int rows_per_thread = (total_rows + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x; // not great Better: have each warp handle one or more rows. int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int num_warps = blockDim.x / 32; // 8 for 256 threads for (int row = warp_id; row < num_valid_in_h; row += num_warps) { if (lane < num_valid_in_w) { smem[row * TILE_W_IN_PADDED + lane] = inp_n_c[(h_in_start + row) * W + (w_in_start + lane)]; } // Handle remaining columns if num_valid_in_w > 32. Since num_valid_in_w <= 32 (TILE_W_IN=32=8*4), and 128*64 case has W_out=31 < 32, wait, num_valid_in_w can be up to 32. So lane 0..31 covers it. } But for the last tile, num_valid_in_w could be 28. So with warp-based row loading, each warp handles one row at a time. 32 threads in a warp load 32 consecutive floats (or fewer for boundary). This is perfectly coalesced. We need up to 32 rows. With 8 warps per block, we need 4 iterations (32/8 = 4). Since num_valid_in_h <= 32, and blockDim.x=256 (8 warps), 4 iterations suffice. Total shared memory stores = 32*32 = 1024 per block. Let me implement this. ```cuda __shared__ float smem[TILE_H_IN * TILE_W_IN_PADDED]; int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int num_warps = blockDim.x / 32; for (int row = warp_id; row < num_valid_in_h; row += num_warps) { int global_h = h_in_start + row; int global_w = w_in_start + lane; if (lane < num_valid_in_w) { smem[row * TILE_W_IN_PADDED + lane] = inp_n_c[global_h * W + global_w]; } } __syncthreads(); ``` This is much cleaner and guarantees coalesced loads (within a warp, all lanes read consecutive addresses in global memory, as long as lane < num_valid_in_w). And shared memory writes are also coalesced (consecutive lanes write to consecutive positions, which are in consecutive banks). Actually, since num_valid_in_w is at most 32 and lane ranges 0..31, all valid lanes participate. The condition `lane < num_valid_in_w` might cause divergence but only at the warp level for the last row of boundary tiles. Actually, each warp handles one row. If num_valid_in_w = 28, then in every warp, lanes 28..31 are disabled. That's within-warp divergence but only 4 lanes out of 32, which is fine. And since it's the same for all warps (num_valid_in_w doesn't change per row for a given tile), the divergence is uniform across warps. Wait, `num_valid_in_w` depends on the tile, not the row. Now I need to make sure the memory layout in shared memory is correct. `smem[row * TILE_W_IN_PADDED + col]`. With TILE_W_IN_PADDED=33, this avoids the most severe bank conflicts. Let me code the full kernel. But before that, another thought: we can unroll the dh/dw loops in the compute phase to reduce loop overhead. For the stats kernel, I can also rewrite it to be cleaner. Actually, instead of two separate kernels, I could try to fuse the stats computation into the main kernel, but since stats need reduction across spatial dimensions, it's better kept separate. Let's write the full code now. One more thing: `clamp_min` and `clamp_max` are passed as float. In the old kernel we applied clamp before maxpool. This matches the reference: clamp is applied after maxpool. Wait, the reference does: x = maxpool(x) x = clamp(x, min, max) So clamp is after maxpool. In the fused kernel, I do max_val = max over the pool, then clamp the max_val. That matches the reference. In my earlier computation of `y` before max, I was applying clamp to each element before taking max. That is WRONG! It should be: find the max of the unclamped elements, then clamp the result. Wait, but clamp is a monotonic function (for max, since clamp truncates at max and min). For maxpool, max(clamp(x_i)) is NOT necessarily equal to clamp(max(x_i))? Actually: max(clamp(x_i, min, max)) vs clamp(max(x_i), min, max). Are they equal? If max(x_i) > max_val, then clamp(max(x_i)) = max_val. And clamp(x_i) for all i is <= max_val, so max(clamp(x_i)) <= max_val. But could it be strictly less? If some x_j = max(x_i) > max_val, then clamp(x_j) = max_val. So max(clamp(x_i)) = max_val. If max(x_i) < min_val, then clamp(max(x_i)) = min_val. And for all i, clamp(x_i) <= min_val, with at least one equal to min_val (the max one). So max(clamp(x_i)) = min_val. If min_val <= max(x_i) <= max_val, then clamp doesn't change anything. So yes, max(clamp(x_i)) = clamp(max(x_i)). This allows us to clamp either before or after the max. However, due to floating point, they should be equivalent since clamp and max are deterministic. But to be safe, we can clamp after max. This saves a few fminf/fmaxf calls (only 1 instead of 16). So in the compute kernel: ```cuda float max_val = -FLT_MAX; #pragma unroll for (int dh = 0; dh < POOL; ++dh) { for (int dw = 0; dw < POOL; ++dw) { float x = smem[...]; float y = x * a + offs; if (y > max_val) max_val = y; } } max_val = fminf(fmaxf(max_val, clamp_min), clamp_max); ``` That's better. Let me write the complete CUDA code now. Actually, I notice that in my previous v2 code, I was applying clamp inside the max loop. That was incorrect? Wait, mathematically it's the same for max. So it shouldn't affect correctness. The previous correctness test passed, so it's fine. But doing it after max is slightly faster. Now, one final optimization: in the compute phase, since `a` and `offs` are constants per channel, we can compute them once. The shared memory gives us `x` values. The computation is simple. Let me write the kernel. Wait, there's one more thing: `blockIdx.z` is limited to 65535. `num_tiles` = 16. So blockIdx.z = 16, well within limits. Complete code: ```cuda #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <float.h> #define TILE_H 8 #define TILE_W 8 #define POOL_SIZE 4 #define TILE_H_IN (TILE_H * POOL_SIZE) #define TILE_W_IN (TILE_W * POOL_SIZE) #define TILE_W_PAD 33 __inline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); } return val; } __inline__ __device__ float block_reduce_sum(float val) { __shared__ float shared[32]; int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int wid = threadIdx.x / 32; val = warp_reduce_sum(val); if (lane == 0) shared[wid] = val; __syncthreads(); val = (threadIdx.x < (blockDim.x + 31) / 32) ? shared[lane] : 0.0f; if (wid == 0) val = warp_reduce_sum(val); return val; } __global__ void compute_group_stats_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ mean, float* __restrict__ inv_std, int N, int C, int H, int W, int num_groups, float eps) { int n = blockIdx.x / num_groups; int g = blockIdx.x % num_groups; int tid = threadIdx.x; int c_per_group = C / num_groups; int count = c_per_group * H * W; const float* group_ptr = input + ((n * C + g * c_per_group) * H) * W; int vec_count = count / 4; int tail_start = vec_count * 4; float sum = 0.0f; for (int i = tid; i < vec_count; i += blockDim.x) { float4 vec = __ldg(reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(group_ptr + i * 4)); sum += vec.x + vec.y + vec.z + vec.w; } __syncthreads(); float m = s_mean; float sum_sq = 0.0f; for (int i = tid; i < vec_count; i += blockDim.x) { float4 vec = __ldg(reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(group_ptr + i * 4)); float d0 = vec.x - m; sum_sq += d0 * d0; float d1 = vec.y - m; sum_sq += d1 * d1; float d2 = vec.z - m; sum_sq += d2 * d2; float d3 = vec.w - m; sum_sq += d3 * d3; } for (int i = tid + tail_start; i < count; i += blockDim.x) { float d = group_ptr[i] - m; sum_sq += d * d; } sum_sq = block_reduce_sum(sum_sq); if (tid == 0) { float var = sum_sq / count; inv_std[blockIdx.x] = rsqrtf(var + eps); } } __global__ void fuse_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ mean, const float* __restrict__ inv_std, const float* __restrict__ gamma, const float* __restrict__ beta, const float* __restrict__ scale, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out, int num_groups, int pool_size, float clamp_min, float clamp_max) { int n = blockIdx.x; int c = blockIdx.y; int tile_id = blockIdx.z; int tiles_w = (W_out + TILE_W - 1) / TILE_W; int tile_h = tile_id / tiles_w; int tile_w = tile_id % tiles_w; int h_start = tile_h * TILE_H; int w_start = tile_w * TILE_W; int num_valid_h = min(TILE_H, H_out - h_start); int num_valid_w = min(TILE_W, W_out - w_start); int num_valid_in_h = num_valid_h * pool_size; int num_valid_in_w = num_valid_w * pool_size; int h_in_start = h_start * pool_size; int w_in_start = w_start * pool_size; __shared__ float smem[TILE_H_IN * TILE_W_PAD]; const float* __restrict__ inp_ptr = input + ((n * C + c) * H) * W; // Load shared memory with coalesced global reads int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int num_warps = blockDim.x / 32; for (int row = warp_id; row < num_valid_in_h; row += num_warps) { int global_h = h_in_start + row; if (lane < num_valid_in_w) { int global_w = w_in_start + lane; int local_h_start = out_h * pool_size; int local_w_start = out_w * pool_size; float max_val = -FLT_MAX; #pragma unroll for (int dh = 0; dh < pool_size; ++dh) { int row_off = (local_h_start + dh) * TILE_W_PAD; #pragma unroll for (int dw = 0; dw < pool_size; ++dw) { float x = smem[row_off + (local_w_start + dw)]; float y = x * a + offs; if (y > max_val) max_val = y; } } max_val = fminf(fmaxf(max_val, clamp_min), clamp_max); int out_idx = ((n * C + c) * H_out + (h_start + out_h)) * W_out + (w_start + out_w); output[out_idx] = max_val; } } void forward(...) { // same as before int tiles_h = (H_out + TILE_H - 1) / TILE_H; int tiles_w = (W_out + TILE_W - 1) / TILE_W; int num_tiles = tiles_h * tiles_w; dim3 block2(256); dim3 grid2(N, C, num_tiles); fuse_kernel<<<grid2, block2, TILE_H_IN * TILE_W_PAD * sizeof(float)>>>(...); } ``` Wait, I need to be careful about `blockIdx.z` limit. grid2.z = num_tiles. For our case, num_tiles=16, fine. For general cases, if W_out and H_out are large, num_tiles could exceed 65535? Let's check: TILE_H=8, TILE_W=8. num_tiles = ceil(H_out/8)*ceil(W_out/8). For H_out=W_out=1024, num_tiles = 128*128 = 16384. That's under 65535. For 4096, it's 512*512 = 262144, which exceeds 65535. To be safe for larger sizes, we should use a 1D grid and compute n, c, tile_id from blockIdx.x. But for this specific problem, num_tiles is small. I'd rather implement the general 1D grid to be robust. 1D grid: int total_blocks = N * C * num_tiles; int block_id = blockIdx.x; int tile_id = block_id % num_tiles; int tmp = block_id / num_tiles; int c = tmp % C; int n = tmp / C; This is safer. Let me rewrite with 1D grid for the fuse kernel. Grid size = N * C * num_tiles. Let me adjust the code. One more thing: I should add `__launch_bounds__` to the fuse kernel to hint register usage. With 256 threads and small shared memory, occupancy is not register-limited, but we can add `__launch_bounds__(256)` just in case. Also, for block_reduce_sum, I fixed the condition `threadIdx.x < (blockDim.x + 31) / 32`. This is correct. Now, let me write the final Python module. Actually, I realize I need to make sure the compile works with the new code. Let me be careful with all the CUDA syntax. One potential issue: `dim3 grid2(N, C, num_tiles);` vs 1D grid. I'll use 1D. Let me write it.

turn 10 · 1 tool call(s) · 67.0s · 43,005 chars (click to expand)
turn 10llm latency: 67.0s
reasoning
The shared memory approach regressed. The coalescing got worse, which means the load pattern isn't good. The main issue with the previous simple kernel was uncoalesced loads. The profile said 24.4x sectors/req, meaning each warp is loading data from 24 different cache sectors per request, which is terrible. Why? Because in the simple kernel, threads in a warp have different `c` (channel) values, but similar `h_out` and `w_out`. So they all access the same spatial location but different channels: - Thread 0: channel 0, H*W offset = 0 - Thread 1: channel 1, H*W offset = H*W - Thread 2: channel 2, H*W offset = 2*H*W These are H*W apart = 16384 floats = 64KB apart. A cache sector is 32 bytes, so consecutive channels are 2000 sectors apart. This means each thread in a warp accesses a different sector => 32 sectors/req. That's exactly what we see (24-27 sectors/req). The ideal is to have each warp process a contiguous region in memory for spatial locality. The key insight is: for each output pixel, we need to read a pool_size*pool_size window from the input, but the memory layout is NCHW, so channels are far apart. But after pooling, each output channel is independent. The most efficient memory access would be: - A warp processes 32 consecutive elements (or 8 consecutive elements with 4 threads each) along the channel dimension for a given spatial location? No, that still has bad spatial locality. Actually, there's a better insight: the output of the conv is NCHW where N=128, C=64, H=W=126. For each sample in the batch and each channel, the spatial data is contiguous. So: - Consecutive threads should access spatially adjacent data within the SAME channel. - For max pooling, adjacent output pixels in the same channel access adjacent input pixels. So the block/thread configuration should be: each block processes one channel for one (or more) output tiles, and threads within the block handle adjacent spatial pixels. Let me redesign: - Each block = one channel, one spatial tile - X threads handling adjacent W_out pixels, Y threads handling adjacent H_out pixels - Thread (tx,ty) processes output pixel (h_start+ty, w_start+tx) For a 2D block of 32x8 = 256 threads: - Block processes a tile of W_out=32, H_out=8 pixels for one channel - Thread tx handles width index, ty handles height index - All threads access the same channel, so the base pointer is the same - Adjacent threads (same ty, tx and tx+1) access adjacent output columns, which means adjacent input columns (within the pool window) - This gives coalesced memory access! Let's redo the kernel with this layout. Block size: 32x8 = 256 threads (dim3(32, 8)) Grid: (num_channel_tiles, num_h_tiles, N) where channels are handled in the x-dimension of the grid, h_tiles in y, batch in z. Wait, a better grid layout: - gridDim.x = number of output tiles in width * number of channels? No. Let's think clearly: For each (n, c), we have H_out x W_out output pixels. We can tile in H_out and W_out. Block: 32 threads in x (W_out direction), 8 threads in y (H_out direction) = 256 threads. Each block processes 32x8 output pixels for one (n,c). Grid: - gridDim.x = number of W_tiles * number of C values processed per... Actually, we need to iterate over N and C. Simplest: - gridDim.x = C * num_w_tiles (one horizontal tile per channel) - gridDim.y = N * num_h_tiles But that limits parallelism to 2D. Better: - gridDim.x = num_w_tiles - gridDim.y = num_h_tiles - gridDim.z = N * C But CUDA grids have a limit on z dimension (65535), and N*C = 128*64 = 8192, which fits. Actually, on modern GPUs, launching 8192 blocks in z is fine. So: - blockIdx.z gives n*C + c - blockIdx.y gives h_tile - blockIdx.x gives w_tile Within the block: - threadIdx.x (0-31) handles w position within the tile - threadIdx.y (0-7) handles h position within the tile This means: - thread (tx, ty) accesses output at (h_tile*8 + ty, w_tile*32 + tx) - For pool=4, it reads from input at (h_base + ty*4, w_base + tx*4), spanning a 4x4 window - All threads in a warp (same ty, tx=0..31) access input pixels in the same row but adjacent starting columns: w_base + tx*4 - Wait, they each access a 4-column window starting at w_base + tx*4, so thread tx accesses columns [w_base+tx*4, w_base+tx*4+3] Are these accesses coalesced? Thread 0 accesses columns [0,1,2,3] Thread 1 accesses columns [4,5,6,7] ... Thread 31 accesses columns [124,125,126,127] Within a warp, thread 0's first load is from address base+0, thread 1 from base+4, thread 2 from base+8, etc. Each float is 4 bytes, so thread k accesses base + k*16 bytes. A cache line/sector is 32 bytes, so thread 0 and 1 are in different sectors (0-7 vs 16-23). Actually: - Thread 0 accesses [0, 16) bytes for its first 4 floats - Thread 1 accesses [16, 32) bytes - Thread 2 accesses [32, 48) bytes - ... So in groups of 2, threads share sectors: - Sector 0: thread 0 (bytes 0-15) and thread 1 (bytes 16-31)? No, 32B sector boundary is at 0, 32, 64... - Thread 0: bytes 0-15 -> sector 0 - Thread 1: bytes 16-31 -> sector 0 - Thread 2: bytes 32-47 -> sector 1 - Thread 3: bytes 48-63 -> sector 1 So 2 threads per sector for the first float, but then each thread needs 4 floats: - Thread 0: [0, 16) -> sector 0 - Thread 1: [16, 32) -> sector 0 - Thread 2: [32, 48) -> sector 1 - Thread 3: [48, 64) -> sector 1 So for 32 threads loading 4 floats each = 128 floats = 512 bytes. 512 / 32 = 16 sectors. 16 sectors / 32 threads = 0.5 sectors/req? No, wait. Actually a warp issues one memory request. If all 32 threads access contiguous 512 bytes spanning 16 sectors, the hardware can coalesce this into roughly 16 sector requests. So sectors per request = 16, for 32 threads = 0.5? No, sectors/req measures how many 32-byte sectors are accessed per warp-wide memory request. Ideal is 1 sector for 32 threads accessing the same 32 bytes, or 2 sectors if accessing 64 bytes, etc. For 128 floats = 512 bytes = 16 sectors, and 32 threads participating, we'd get 16 sectors per request for the first load. But each thread does 4 loads (one for each column in its pool window), and the loads are strided. Actually, thread tx accesses 4 floats at w_base + tx*4, w_base + tx*4+1, w_base + tx*4+2, w_base + tx*4+3. For tx=0 to 31, this covers the range [w_base, w_base + 127]. But here tx=0 accesses bank 0 at address (row, 0), while tx=8 accesses bank 0 at address (row, 32). These are different addresses in the same bank, causing a 2-way conflict.
